[Basics tutorial](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/)\n\n\n# Basics tutorial\nA basic tutorial introduction to gRPC in Dart.\n[Contents](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#td-content__toc)\n  *     * [Why use gRPC?](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#why-use-grpc)\n    * [Example code and setup](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#example-code-and-setup)\n    * [Defining the service](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#defining-the-service)\n    * [Generating client and server code](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#generating-client-and-server-code)\n    * [Creating the server](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#server)\n      * [Implementing RouteGuide](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#implementing-routeguide)\n        * [Simple RPC](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#simple-rpc)\n        * [Server-side streaming RPC](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#server-side-streaming-rpc)\n        * [Client-side streaming RPC](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#client-side-streaming-rpc)\n        * [Bidirectional streaming RPC](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#bidirectional-streaming-rpc)\n      * [Starting the server](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#starting-the-server)\n    * [Creating the client](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#client)\n      * [Creating a stub](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#creating-a-stub)\n      * [Calling service methods](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#calling-service-methods)\n        * [Simple RPC](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#simple-rpc-1)\n        * [Server-side streaming RPC](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#server-side-streaming-rpc-1)\n        * [Client-side streaming RPC](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#client-side-streaming-rpc-1)\n        * [Bidirectional streaming RPC](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#bidirectional-streaming-rpc-1)\n    * [Try it out!](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#try-it-out)\n    * [Reporting issues](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#reporting-issues)\n\n\n# Basics tutorial\nA basic tutorial introduction to gRPC in Dart.\nThis tutorial provides a basic Dart programmer’s introduction to working with gRPC.\nBy walking through this example you’ll learn how to:\n  * Define a service in a `.proto` file.\n  * Generate server and client code using the protocol buffer compiler.\n  * Use the Dart gRPC API to write a simple client and server for your service.\n\n\nIt assumes that you have read the [Introduction to gRPC](https://grpc.io/docs/what-is-grpc/introduction/) and are familiar with [protocol buffers](https://protobuf.dev/overview). Note that the example in this tutorial uses the proto3 version of the protocol buffers language: you can find out more in the [proto3 language guide](https://protobuf.dev/programming-guides/proto3).\n### Why use gRPC?[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#why-use-grpc)\nOur example is a simple route mapping application that lets clients get information about features on their route, create a summary of their route, and exchange route information such as traffic updates with the server and other clients.\nWith gRPC we can define our service once in a `.proto` file and generate clients and servers in any of gRPC’s supported languages, which in turn can be run in environments ranging from servers inside a large data center to your own tablet — all the complexity of communication between different languages and environments is handled for you by gRPC. We also get all the advantages of working with protocol buffers, including efficient serialization, a simple IDL, and easy interface updating.\n### Example code and setup[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#example-code-and-setup)\nThe example code for our tutorial is in [grpc/grpc-dart/example/route_guide](https://github.com/grpc/grpc-dart/tree/master/example/route_guide). To download the example, clone the `grpc-dart` repository by running the following command:\n```\ngit clone --depth 1 https://github.com/grpc/grpc-dart\n\n```\n\nThen change your current directory to `grpc-dart/example/route_guide`:\n```\ncd grpc-dart/example/route_guide\n\n```\n\nYou should have already installed the tools needed to generate client and server interface code – if you haven’t, see [Quick start](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/quickstart/) for setup instructions.\n### Defining the service[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#defining-the-service)\nOur first step (as you’ll know from the [Introduction to gRPC](https://grpc.io/docs/what-is-grpc/introduction/)) is to define the gRPC _service_ and the method _request_ and _response_ types using [protocol buffers](https://protobuf.dev/overview). You can see the complete `.proto` file in [`example/route_guide/protos/route_guide.proto`](https://github.com/grpc/grpc-dart/blob/master/example/route_guide/protos/route_guide.proto).\nTo define a service, you specify a named `service` in your `.proto` file:\n```\nservice RouteGuide {  ...}\n```\n\nThen you define `rpc` methods inside your service definition, specifying their request and response types. gRPC lets you define four kinds of service method, all of which are used in the `RouteGuide` service:\n  * A _simple RPC_ where the client sends a request to the server using the stub and waits for a response to come back, just like a normal function call.\n```\n// Obtains the feature at a given position.\nrpc GetFeature(Point) returns (Feature) {}\n```\n\n  * A _server-side streaming RPC_ where the client sends a request to the server and gets a stream to read a sequence of messages back. The client reads from the returned stream until there are no more messages. As you can see in our example, you specify a server-side streaming method by placing the `stream` keyword before the _response_ type.\n```\n// Obtains the Features available within the given Rectangle. Results are\n// streamed rather than returned at once (e.g. in a response message with a\n// repeated field), as the rectangle may cover a large area and contain a\n// huge number of features.\nrpc ListFeatures(Rectangle) returns (stream Feature) {}\n```\n\n  * A _client-side streaming RPC_ where the client writes a sequence of messages and sends them to the server, again using a provided stream. Once the client has finished writing the messages, it waits for the server to read them all and return its response. You specify a client-side streaming method by placing the `stream` keyword before the _request_ type.\n```\n// Accepts a stream of Points on a route being traversed, returning a\n// RouteSummary when traversal is completed.\nrpc RecordRoute(stream Point) returns (RouteSummary) {}\n```\n\n  * A _bidirectional streaming RPC_ where both sides send a sequence of messages using a read-write stream. The two streams operate independently, so clients and servers can read and write in whatever order they like: for example, the server could wait to receive all the client messages before writing its responses, or it could alternately read a message then write a message, or some other combination of reads and writes. The order of messages in each stream is preserved. You specify this type of method by placing the `stream` keyword before both the request and the response.\n```\n// Accepts a stream of RouteNotes sent while a route is being traversed,\n// while receiving other RouteNotes (e.g. from other users).\nrpc RouteChat(stream RouteNote) returns (stream RouteNote) {}\n```\n\n\n\nOur `.proto` file also contains protocol buffer message type definitions for all the request and response types used in our service methods - for example, here’s the `Point` message type:\n```\n// Points are represented as latitude-longitude pairs in the E7 representation\n// (degrees multiplied by 10**7 and rounded to the nearest integer).\n// Latitudes should be in the range +/- 90 degrees and longitude should be in\n// the range +/- 180 degrees (inclusive).\nmessage Point { int32 latitude = 1; int32 longitude = 2;}\n```\n\n### Generating client and server code[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#generating-client-and-server-code)\nNext we need to generate the gRPC client and server interfaces from our `.proto` service definition. We do this using the protocol buffer compiler `protoc` with a special Dart plugin. This is similar to what we did in the [Quick start](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/quickstart/).\nFrom the `route_guide` example directory run:\n```\nprotoc -I protos/ protos/route_guide.proto --dart_out=grpc:lib/src/generated\n\n```\n\nRunning this command generates the following files in the `lib/src/generated` directory under the `route_guide` example directory:\n  * `route_guide.pb.dart`\n  * `route_guide.pbenum.dart`\n  * `route_guide.pbgrpc.dart`\n  * `route_guide.pbjson.dart`\n\n\nThis contains:\n  * All the protocol buffer code to populate, serialize, and retrieve our request and response message types\n  * An interface type (or _stub_) for clients to call with the methods defined in the `RouteGuide` service.\n  * An interface type for servers to implement, also with the methods defined in the `RouteGuide` service.\n\n\n### Creating the server[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#server)\nFirst let’s look at how we create a `RouteGuide` server. If you’re only interested in creating gRPC clients, you can skip this section and go straight to [Creating the client](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#client) (though you might find it interesting anyway!).\nThere are two parts to making our `RouteGuide` service do its job:\n  * Implementing the service interface generated from our service definition: doing the actual “work” of our service.\n  * Running a gRPC server to listen for requests from clients and dispatch them to the right service implementation.\n\n\nYou can find our example `RouteGuide` server in [grpc-dart/example/route_guide/lib/src/server.dart](https://github.com/grpc/grpc-dart/tree/master/example/route_guide/lib/src/server.dart). Let’s take a closer look at how it works.\n#### Implementing RouteGuide[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#implementing-routeguide)\nAs you can see, our server has a `RouteGuideService` class that extends the generated abstract `RouteGuideServiceBase` class:\n```\nclass RouteGuideService extends RouteGuideServiceBase {\n Future<Feature> getFeature(grpc.ServiceCall call, Point request) async {\n  ...\n }\n Stream<Feature> listFeatures(\n   grpc.ServiceCall call, Rectangle request) async* {\n  ...\n }\n Future<RouteSummary> recordRoute(\n   grpc.ServiceCall call, Stream<Point> request) async {\n  ...\n }\n Stream<RouteNote> routeChat(\n   grpc.ServiceCall call, Stream<RouteNote> request) async* {\n  ...\n }\n ...\n}\n\n```\n\n##### Simple RPC[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#simple-rpc)\n`RouteGuideService` implements all our service methods. Let’s look at the simplest type first, `GetFeature`, which just gets a `Point` from the client and returns the corresponding feature information from its database in a `Feature`.\n```\n/// GetFeature handler. Returns a feature for the given location.\n/// The [context] object provides access to client metadata, cancellation, etc.\n@override\nFuture<Feature> getFeature(grpc.ServiceCall call, Point request) async {\n return featuresDb.firstWhere((f) => f.location == request,\n   orElse: () => Feature()..location = request);\n}\n\n```\n\nThe method is passed a context object for the RPC and the client’s `Point` protocol buffer request. It returns a `Feature` protocol buffer object with the response information. In the method we populate the `Feature` with the appropriate information, and then `return` it to the gRPC framework, which sends it back to the client.\n##### Server-side streaming RPC[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#server-side-streaming-rpc)\nNow let’s look at one of our streaming RPCs. `ListFeatures` is a server-side streaming RPC, so we need to send back multiple `Feature`s to our client.\n```\n/// ListFeatures handler. Returns a stream of features within the given\n/// rectangle.\n@override\nStream<Feature> listFeatures(\n  grpc.ServiceCall call, Rectangle request) async* {\n final normalizedRectangle = _normalize(request);\n // For each feature, check if it is in the given bounding box\n for (var feature in featuresDb) {\n  if (feature.name.isEmpty) continue;\n  final location = feature.location;\n  if (_contains(normalizedRectangle, location)) {\n   yield feature;\n  }\n }\n}\n\n```\n\nAs you can see, instead of getting and returning simple request and response objects in our method, this time we get a request object (the `Rectangle` in which our client wants to find `Feature`s) and return a `Stream` of `Feature` objects.\nIn the method, we populate as many `Feature` objects as we need to return, adding them to the returned stream using `yield`. The stream is automatically closed when the method returns, telling gRPC that we have finished writing responses.\nShould any error happen in this call, the error will be added as an exception to the stream, and the gRPC layer will translate it into an appropriate RPC status to be sent on the wire.\n##### Client-side streaming RPC[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#client-side-streaming-rpc)\nNow let’s look at something a little more complicated: the client-side streaming method `RecordRoute`, where we get a stream of `Point`s from the client and return a single `RouteSummary` with information about their trip. As you can see, this time the request parameter is a stream, which the server can use to both read request messages from the client. The server returns its single response just like in the simple RPC case.\n```\n/// RecordRoute handler. Gets a stream of points, and responds with statistics\n/// about the \"trip\": number of points, number of known features visited,\n/// total distance traveled, and total time spent.\n@override\nFuture<RouteSummary> recordRoute(\n  grpc.ServiceCall call, Stream<Point> request) async {\n int pointCount = 0;\n int featureCount = 0;\n double distance = 0.0;\n Point previous;\n final timer = Stopwatch();\n await for (var location in request) {\n  if (!timer.isRunning) timer.start();\n  pointCount++;\n  final feature = featuresDb.firstWhereOrNull((f) => f.location == location);\n  if (feature != null) {\n   featureCount++;\n  }\n  // For each point after the first, add the incremental distance from the\n  // previous point to the total distance value.\n  if (previous != null) distance += _distance(previous, location);\n  previous = location;\n }\n timer.stop();\n return RouteSummary()\n  ..pointCount = pointCount\n  ..featureCount = featureCount\n  ..distance = distance.round()\n  ..elapsedTime = timer.elapsed.inSeconds;\n}\n\n```\n\nIn the method body we use `await for` in the request stream to repeatedly read in our client’s requests (in this case `Point` objects) until there are no more messages. Once the request stream is done, the server can return its `RouteSummary`.\n##### Bidirectional streaming RPC[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#bidirectional-streaming-rpc)\nFinally, let’s look at our bidirectional streaming RPC `RouteChat()`.\n```\n/// RouteChat handler. Receives a stream of message/location pairs, and\n/// responds with a stream of all previous messages at each of those\n/// locations.\n@override\nStream<RouteNote> routeChat(\n  grpc.ServiceCall call, Stream<RouteNote> request) async* {\n await for (var note in request) {\n  final notes = routeNotes.putIfAbsent(note.location, () => <RouteNote>[]);\n  for (var note in notes) yield note;\n  notes.add(note);\n }\n}\n\n```\n\nThis time we get a stream of `RouteNote` that, as in our client-side streaming example, can be used to read messages. However, this time we return values via our method’s returned stream while the client is still writing messages to _their_ message stream.\nThe syntax for reading and writing here is the same as our client-streaming and server-streaming methods. Although each side will always get the other’s messages in the order they were written, both the client and server can read and write in any order — the streams operate completely independently.\n#### Starting the server[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#starting-the-server)\nOnce we’ve implemented all our methods, we also need to start up a gRPC server so that clients can actually use our service. The following snippet shows how we do this for our `RouteGuide` service:\n```\nFuture<void> main(List<String> args) async {\n final server = grpc.Server.create([RouteGuideService()]);\n await server.serve(port: 8080);\n print('Server listening...');\n}\n\n```\n\nTo build and start a server, we:\n  1. Create an instance of the gRPC server using `grpc.Server.create()`, giving a list of service implementations.\n  2. Call `serve()` on the server to start listening for requests, optionally passing in the address and port to listen on. The server will continue to serve requests asynchronously until `shutdown()` is called on it.\n\n\n### Creating the client[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#client)\nIn this section, we’ll look at creating a Dart client for our `RouteGuide` service. The complete client code is available from [grpc-dart/example/route_guide/lib/src/client.dart](https://github.com/grpc/grpc-dart/tree/master/example/route_guide/lib/src/client.dart).\n#### Creating a stub[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#creating-a-stub)\nTo call service methods, we first need to create a gRPC _channel_ to communicate with the server. We create this by passing the server address and port number to `ClientChannel()` as follows:\n```\nfinal channel = ClientChannel('127.0.0.1',\n  port: 8080,\n  options: const ChannelOptions(\n    credentials: ChannelCredentials.insecure()));\n\n```\n\nYou can use `ChannelOptions` to set TLS options (for example, trusted certificates) for the channel, if necessary.\nOnce the gRPC _channel_ is setup, we need a client _stub_ to perform RPCs. We get it by instantiating `RouteGuideClient`, which is provided by the package generated from the example `.proto` file.\n```\nstub = RouteGuideClient(channel,\n  options: CallOptions(timeout: Duration(seconds: 30)));\n\n```\n\nYou can use `CallOptions` to set auth credentials (for example, GCE credentials or JWT credentials) when a service requires them. The `RouteGuide` service doesn’t require any credentials.\n#### Calling service methods[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#calling-service-methods)\nNow let’s look at how we call our service methods. Note that in gRPC-Dart, RPCs are always asynchronous, which means that the RPC returns a `Future` or `Stream` that must be listened to, to get the response from the server or an error.\n##### Simple RPC[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#simple-rpc-1)\nCalling the simple RPC `GetFeature` is nearly as straightforward as calling a local method.\n```\nfinal point = Point()\n ..latitude = 409146138\n ..longitude = -746188906;\nfinal feature = await stub.getFeature(point));\n\n```\n\nAs you can see, we call the method on the stub we got earlier. In our method parameters we pass a request protocol buffer object (in our case `Point`). We can also pass an optional `CallOptions` object which lets us change our RPC’s behavior if necessary, such as time-out. If the call doesn’t return an error, the returned `Future` completes with the response information from the server. If there is an error, the `Future` will complete with the error.\n##### Server-side streaming RPC[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#server-side-streaming-rpc-1)\nHere’s where we call the server-side streaming method `ListFeatures`, which returns a stream of geographical `Feature`s. If you’ve already read [Creating the server](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#server) some of this may look very familiar - streaming RPCs are implemented in a similar way on both sides.\n```\nfinal rect = Rectangle()...; // initialize a Rectangle\ntry {\n await for (var feature in stub.listFeatures(rect)) {\n  print(feature);\n }\ncatch (e) {\n print('ERROR: $e');\n}\n\n```\n\nAs in the simple RPC, we pass the method a request. However, instead of getting a `Future` back, we get a `Stream`. The client can use the stream to read the server’s responses.\nWe use `await for` on the returned stream to repeatedly read in the server’s responses to a response protocol buffer object (in this case a `Feature`) until there are no more messages.\n##### Client-side streaming RPC[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#client-side-streaming-rpc-1)\nThe client-side streaming method `RecordRoute` is similar to the server-side method, except that we pass the method a `Stream` and get a `Future` back.\n```\nfinal random = Random();\n// Generate a number of random points\nStream<Point> generateRoute(int count) async* {\n for (int i = 0; i < count; i++) {\n  final point = featuresDb[random.nextInt(featuresDb.length)].location;\n  yield point;\n }\n}\nfinal pointCount = random.nextInt(100) + 2; // Traverse at least two points\nfinal summary = await stub.recordRoute(generateRoute(pointCount));\nprint('Route summary: $summary');\n\n```\n\nSince the `generateRoute()` method is `async*`, the points will be generated when gRPC listens to the request stream and sends the point messages to the server. Once the stream is done (when `generateRoute()` returns), gRPC knows that we’ve finished writing and are expecting to receive a response. The returned `Future` will either complete with the `RouteSummary` message received from the server, or an error.\n##### Bidirectional streaming RPC[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#bidirectional-streaming-rpc-1)\nFinally, let’s look at our bidirectional streaming RPC `RouteChat()`. As in the case of `RecordRoute`, we pass the method a stream where we will write the request messages, and like in `ListFeatures`, we get back a stream that we can use to read the response messages. However, this time we will send values via our method’s stream while the server is also writing messages to _their_ message stream.\n```\nStream<RouteNote> outgoingNotes = ...;\nfinal responses = stub.routeChat(outgoingNotes);\nawait for (var note in responses) {\n print('Got message ${note.message} at ${note.location.latitude}, ${note\n   .location.longitude}');\n}\n\n```\n\nThe syntax for reading and writing here is very similar to our client-side and server-side streaming methods. Although each side will always get the other’s messages in the order they were written, both the client and server can read and write in any order — the streams operate completely independently.\n### Try it out![ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#try-it-out)\nWork from the example directory:\n```\ncd example/route_guide\n\n```\n\nGet packages:\n```\ndart pub get\n\n```\n\nRun the server:\n```\ndart bin/server.dart\n\n```\n\nFrom a different terminal, run the client:\n```\ndart bin/client.dart\n\n```\n\n### Reporting issues[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/dart/basics/#reporting-issues)\nIf you find a problem with Dart gRPC, please [file an issue](https://github.com/grpc/grpc-dart/issues/new) in our issue tracker.\nLast [C# / .NET](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/csharp/)\n\n\n# C# / .NET\n _This page used to contain the documentation for the original C# implementation of gRPC based on the native gRPC Core library (i.e.`Grpc.Core` nuget package). The implementation is currently in maintenance mode and its source code has been [moved](https://github.com/grpc/grpc/blob/master/src/csharp/README.md). We plan to deprecate the implementation in the future (see [blogpost](https://grpc.io/blog/grpc-csharp-future/)) and we recommend that all users use the [grpc-dotnet](https://github.com/grpc/grpc-dotnet) implementation instead._\nThe following pages cover the C# implementation of gRPC for .NET ([grpc-dotnet](https://github.com/grpc/grpc-dotnet)):\n  * [Introduction to gRPC on .NET Core](https://docs.microsoft.com/aspnet/core/grpc)\n  * [Tutorial: Create a gRPC client and server in ASP.NET Core](https://docs.microsoft.com/aspnet/core/tutorials/grpc/grpc-start)\n\n\nSeveral sample applications are available from the [examples](https://github.com/grpc/grpc-dotnet/tree/master/examples) folder in the [grpc-dotnet](https://github.com/grpc/grpc-dotnet) repository.\n##### [API reference (legacy Grpc.Core only)](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/csharp/api/)\n##### [Daily builds (Grpc.Tools [Basics tutorial](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/)\n\n\n# Basics tutorial\nA basic tutorial introduction to gRPC in C++.\n[Contents](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#td-content__toc)\n  *     * [Why use gRPC?](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#why-use-grpc)\n    * [Example code and setup](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#example-code-and-setup)\n    * [Defining the service](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#defining-the-service)\n    * [Generating client and server code](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#generating-client-and-server-code)\n    * [Creating the server](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#server)\n      * [Implementing RouteGuide](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#implementing-routeguide)\n      * [Starting the server](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#starting-the-server)\n    * [Creating the client](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#client)\n      * [Creating a stub](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#creating-a-stub)\n      * [Calling service methods](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#calling-service-methods)\n        * [Simple RPC](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#simple-rpc)\n        * [Streaming RPCs](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#streaming-rpcs)\n    * [Try it out!](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#try-it-out)\n\n\n# Basics tutorial\nA basic tutorial introduction to gRPC in C++.\nThis tutorial provides a basic C++ programmer’s introduction to working with gRPC.\nBy walking through this example you’ll learn how to:\n  * Define a service in a `.proto` file.\n  * Generate server and client code using the protocol buffer compiler.\n  * Use the C++ gRPC API to write a simple client and server for your service.\n\n\nIt assumes that you have read the [Introduction to gRPC](https://grpc.io/docs/what-is-grpc/introduction/) and are familiar with [protocol buffers](https://protobuf.dev/overview). Note that the example in this tutorial uses the proto3 version of the protocol buffers language: you can find out more in the [proto3 language guide](https://protobuf.dev/programming-guides/proto3) and [C++ generated code guide](https://protobuf.dev/reference/cpp/cpp-generated).\n### Why use gRPC?[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#why-use-grpc)\nOur example is a simple route mapping application that lets clients get information about features on their route, create a summary of their route, and exchange route information such as traffic updates with the server and other clients.\nWith gRPC we can define our service once in a `.proto` file and generate clients and servers in any of gRPC’s supported languages, which in turn can be run in environments ranging from servers inside a large data center to your own tablet — all the complexity of communication between different languages and environments is handled for you by gRPC. We also get all the advantages of working with protocol buffers, including efficient serialization, a simple IDL, and easy interface updating.\n### Example code and setup[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#example-code-and-setup)\nThe example code is part of the `grpc` repo under [examples/cpp/route_guide](https://github.com/grpc/grpc/tree/v1.73.0/examples/cpp/route_guide). Get the example code and build gRPC:\n  1. Follow the Quick start instructions to [build and locally install gRPC from source](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#install-grpc).\n  2. From the repo folder, change to the route guide example directory:\n```\ncd examples/cpp/route_guide\n\n```\n\n  3. Run `cmake`\n```\nmkdir -p cmake/build\ncd cmake/build\ncmake -DCMAKE_PREFIX_PATH=$MY_INSTALL_DIR ../..\n\n```\n\n\n\n### Defining the service[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#defining-the-service)\nOur first step (as you’ll know from the [Introduction to gRPC](https://grpc.io/docs/what-is-grpc/introduction/)) is to define the gRPC _service_ and the method _request_ and _response_ types using [protocol buffers](https://protobuf.dev/overview). You can see the complete `.proto` file in [`examples/protos/route_guide.proto`](https://github.com/grpc/grpc/blob/v1.73.0/examples/protos/route_guide.proto).\nTo define a service, you specify a named `service` in your `.proto` file:\n```\nservice RouteGuide {  ...}\n```\n\nThen you define `rpc` methods inside your service definition, specifying their request and response types. gRPC lets you define four kinds of service method, all of which are used in the `RouteGuide` service:\n  * A _simple RPC_ where the client sends a request to the server using the stub and waits for a response to come back, just like a normal function call.\n```\n// Obtains the feature at a given position.\nrpc GetFeature(Point) returns (Feature) {}\n```\n\n  * A _server-side streaming RPC_ where the client sends a request to the server and gets a stream to read a sequence of messages back. The client reads from the returned stream until there are no more messages. As you can see in our example, you specify a server-side streaming method by placing the `stream` keyword before the _response_ type.\n```\n// Obtains the Features available within the given Rectangle. Results are\n// streamed rather than returned at once (e.g. in a response message with a\n// repeated field), as the rectangle may cover a large area and contain a\n// huge number of features.\nrpc ListFeatures(Rectangle) returns (stream Feature) {}\n```\n\n  * A _client-side streaming RPC_ where the client writes a sequence of messages and sends them to the server, again using a provided stream. Once the client has finished writing the messages, it waits for the server to read them all and return its response. You specify a client-side streaming method by placing the `stream` keyword before the _request_ type.\n```\n// Accepts a stream of Points on a route being traversed, returning a\n// RouteSummary when traversal is completed.\nrpc RecordRoute(stream Point) returns (RouteSummary) {}\n```\n\n  * A _bidirectional streaming RPC_ where both sides send a sequence of messages using a read-write stream. The two streams operate independently, so clients and servers can read and write in whatever order they like: for example, the server could wait to receive all the client messages before writing its responses, or it could alternately read a message then write a message, or some other combination of reads and writes. The order of messages in each stream is preserved. You specify this type of method by placing the `stream` keyword before both the request and the response.\n```\n// Accepts a stream of RouteNotes sent while a route is being traversed,\n// while receiving other RouteNotes (e.g. from other users).\nrpc RouteChat(stream RouteNote) returns (stream RouteNote) {}\n```\n\n\n\nOur `.proto` file also contains protocol buffer message type definitions for all the request and response types used in our service methods - for example, here’s the `Point` message type:\n```\n// Points are represented as latitude-longitude pairs in the E7 representation\n// (degrees multiplied by 10**7 and rounded to the nearest integer).\n// Latitudes should be in the range +/- 90 degrees and longitude should be in\n// the range +/- 180 degrees (inclusive).\nmessage Point { int32 latitude = 1; int32 longitude = 2;}\n```\n\n### Generating client and server code[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#generating-client-and-server-code)\nNext we need to generate the gRPC client and server interfaces from our `.proto` service definition. We do this using the protocol buffer compiler `protoc` with a special gRPC C++ plugin.\nFor simplicity, we’ve provided a [CMakeLists.txt](https://github.com/grpc/grpc/blob/v1.73.0/examples/cpp/route_guide/CMakeLists.txt) that runs `protoc` for you with the appropriate plugin, input, and output (if you want to run this yourself, make sure you’ve installed protoc and followed the gRPC code [installation instructions](https://github.com/grpc/grpc/blob/v1.73.0/src/cpp/README.md#cmake) first):\n```\nmake route_guide.grpc.pb.o\n\n```\n\nwhich actually runs:\n```\nprotoc -I ../../protos --grpc_out=. --plugin=protoc-gen-grpc=`which grpc_cpp_plugin` ../../protos/route_guide.proto\nprotoc -I ../../protos --cpp_out=. ../../protos/route_guide.proto\n\n```\n\nRunning this command generates the following files in your current directory:\n  * `route_guide.pb.h`, the header which declares your generated message classes\n  * `route_guide.pb.cc`, which contains the implementation of your message classes\n  * `route_guide.grpc.pb.h`, the header which declares your generated service classes\n  * `route_guide.grpc.pb.cc`, which contains the implementation of your service classes\n\n\nThese contain:\n  * All the protocol buffer code to populate, serialize, and retrieve our request and response message types\n  * A class called `RouteGuide` that contains\n    * a remote interface type (or _stub_) for clients to call with the methods defined in the `RouteGuide` service.\n    * two abstract interfaces for servers to implement, also with the methods defined in the `RouteGuide` service.\n\n\n### Creating the server[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#server)\nFirst let’s look at how we create a `RouteGuide` server. If you’re only interested in creating gRPC clients, you can skip this section and go straight to [Creating the client](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#client) (though you might find it interesting anyway!).\nThere are two parts to making our `RouteGuide` service do its job:\n  * Implementing the service interface generated from our service definition: doing the actual “work” of our service.\n  * Running a gRPC server to listen for requests from clients and return the service responses.\n\n\nYou can find our example `RouteGuide` server in [examples/cpp/route_guide/route_guide_server.cc](https://github.com/grpc/grpc/blob/v1.73.0/examples/cpp/route_guide/route_guide_server.cc). Let’s take a closer look at how it works.\n#### Implementing RouteGuide[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#implementing-routeguide)\nAs you can see, our server has a `RouteGuideImpl` class that implements the generated `RouteGuide::Service` interface:\n```\nclass RouteGuideImpl final : public RouteGuide::Service {\n...\n}\n\n```\n\nIn this case we’re implementing the _synchronous_ version of `RouteGuide`, which provides our default gRPC server behaviour. It’s also possible to implement an asynchronous interface, `RouteGuide::AsyncService`, which allows you to further customize your server’s threading behaviour, though we won’t look at this in this tutorial.\n`RouteGuideImpl` implements all our service methods. Let’s look at the simplest type first, `GetFeature`, which just gets a `Point` from the client and returns the corresponding feature information from its database in a `Feature`.\n```\nStatus GetFeature(ServerContext* context, const Point* point,\n         Feature* feature) override {\n feature->set_name(GetFeatureName(*point, feature_list_));\n feature->mutable_location()->CopyFrom(*point);\n return Status::OK;\n}\n\n```\n\nThe method is passed a context object for the RPC, the client’s `Point` protocol buffer request, and a `Feature` protocol buffer to fill in with the response information. In the method we populate the `Feature` with the appropriate information, and then `return` with an `OK` status to tell gRPC that we’ve finished dealing with the RPC and that the `Feature` can be returned to the client.\nNote that all service methods can (and will!) be called from multiple threads at the same time. You have to make sure that your method implementations are thread safe. In our example, `feature_list_` is never changed after construction, so it is safe by design. But if `feature_list_` would change during the lifetime of the service, we would need to synchronize access to this member.\nNow let’s look at something a bit more complicated - a streaming RPC. `ListFeatures` is a server-side streaming RPC, so we need to send back multiple `Feature`s to our client.\n```\nStatus ListFeatures(ServerContext* context, const Rectangle* rectangle,\n          ServerWriter<Feature>* writer) override {\n auto lo = rectangle->lo();\n auto hi = rectangle->hi();\n long left = std::min(lo.longitude(), hi.longitude());\n long right = std::max(lo.longitude(), hi.longitude());\n long top = std::max(lo.latitude(), hi.latitude());\n long bottom = std::min(lo.latitude(), hi.latitude());\n for (const Feature& f : feature_list_) {\n  if (f.location().longitude() >= left &&\n    f.location().longitude() <= right &&\n    f.location().latitude() >= bottom &&\n    f.location().latitude() <= top) {\n   writer->Write(f);\n  }\n }\n return Status::OK;\n}\n\n```\n\nAs you can see, instead of getting simple request and response objects in our method parameters, this time we get a request object (the `Rectangle` in which our client wants to find `Feature`s) and a special `ServerWriter` object. In the method, we populate as many `Feature` objects as we need to return, writing them to the `ServerWriter` using its `Write()` method. Finally, as in our simple RPC, we `return Status::OK` to tell gRPC that we’ve finished writing responses.\nIf you look at the client-side streaming method `RecordRoute` you’ll see it’s quite similar, except this time we get a `ServerReader` instead of a request object and a single response. We use the `ServerReader`s `Read()` method to repeatedly read in our client’s requests to a request object (in this case a `Point`) until there are no more messages: the server needs to check the return value of `Read()` after each call. If `true`, the stream is still good and it can continue reading; if `false` the message stream has ended.\n```\nwhile (stream->Read(&point)) {\n ...//process client input\n}\n\n```\n\nFinally, let’s look at our bidirectional streaming RPC `RouteChat()`.\n```\nStatus RouteChat(ServerContext* context,\n         ServerReaderWriter<RouteNote, RouteNote>* stream) override {\n RouteNote note;\n while (stream->Read(&note)) {\n  std::unique_lock<std::mutex> lock(mu_);\n  for (const RouteNote& n : received_notes_) {\n   if (n.location().latitude() == note.location().latitude() &&\n     n.location().longitude() == note.location().longitude()) {\n    stream->Write(n);\n   }\n  }\n  received_notes_.push_back(note);\n }\n return Status::OK;\n}\n\n```\n\nThis time we get a `ServerReaderWriter` that can be used to read _and_ write messages. The syntax for reading and writing here is exactly the same as for our client-streaming and server-streaming methods. Although each side will always get the other’s messages in the order they were written, both the client and server can read and write in any order — the streams operate completely independently.\nNote that since `received_notes_` is an instance variable and can be accessed by multiple threads, we use a mutex lock here to guarantee exclusive access.\n#### Starting the server[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#starting-the-server)\nOnce we’ve implemented all our methods, we also need to start up a gRPC server so that clients can actually use our service. The following snippet shows how we do this for our `RouteGuide` service:\n```\nvoid RunServer(const std::string& db_path) {\n std::string server_address(\"0.0.0.0:50051\");\n RouteGuideImpl service(db_path);\n ServerBuilder builder;\n builder.AddListeningPort(server_address, grpc::InsecureServerCredentials());\n builder.RegisterService(&service);\n std::unique_ptr<Server> server(builder.BuildAndStart());\n std::cout << \"Server listening on \" << server_address << std::endl;\n server->Wait();\n}\n\n```\n\nAs you can see, we build and start our server using a `ServerBuilder`. To do this, we:\n  1. Create an instance of our service implementation class `RouteGuideImpl`.\n  2. Create an instance of the factory `ServerBuilder` class.\n  3. Specify the address and port we want to use to listen for client requests using the builder’s `AddListeningPort()` method.\n  4. Register our service implementation with the builder.\n  5. Call `BuildAndStart()` on the builder to create and start an RPC server for our service.\n  6. Call `Wait()` on the server to do a blocking wait until process is killed or `Shutdown()` is called.\n\n\n### Creating the client[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#client)\nIn this section, we’ll look at creating a C++ client for our `RouteGuide` service. You can see our complete example client code in [examples/cpp/route_guide/route_guide_client.cc](https://github.com/grpc/grpc/blob/v1.73.0/examples/cpp/route_guide/route_guide_client.cc).\n#### Creating a stub[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#creating-a-stub)\nTo call service methods, we first need to create a _stub_.\nFirst we need to create a gRPC _channel_ for our stub, specifying the server address and port we want to connect to - in our case we’ll use no SSL:\n```\ngrpc::CreateChannel(\"localhost:50051\", grpc::InsecureChannelCredentials());\n\n```\n\n#### Note\nIn order to set additional options for the _channel_ , use the `grpc::CreateCustomChannel()` api with any special channel arguments - `grpc::ChannelArguments`.\nNow we can use the channel to create our stub using the `NewStub` method provided in the `RouteGuide` class we generated from our `.proto`.\n```\npublic:\n RouteGuideClient(std::shared_ptr<ChannelInterface> channel,\n         const std::string& db)\n   : stub_(RouteGuide::NewStub(channel)) {\n  ...\n }\n\n```\n\n#### Calling service methods[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#calling-service-methods)\nNow let’s look at how we call our service methods. Note that in this tutorial we’re calling the _blocking/synchronous_ versions of each method: this means that the RPC call waits for the server to respond, and will either return a response or raise an exception.\n##### Simple RPC[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#simple-rpc)\nCalling the simple RPC `GetFeature` is nearly as straightforward as calling a local method.\n```\nPoint point;\nFeature feature;\npoint = MakePoint(409146138, -746188906);\nGetOneFeature(point, &feature);\n...\nbool GetOneFeature(const Point& point, Feature* feature) {\n ClientContext context;\n Status status = stub_->GetFeature(&context, point, feature);\n ...\n}\n\n```\n\nAs you can see, we create and populate a request protocol buffer object (in our case `Point`), and create a response protocol buffer object for the server to fill in. We also create a `ClientContext` object for our call - you can optionally set RPC configuration values on this object, such as deadlines, though for now we’ll use the default settings. Note that you cannot reuse this object between calls. Finally, we call the method on the stub, passing it the context, request, and response. If the method returns `OK`, then we can read the response information from the server from our response object.\n```\nstd::cout << \"Found feature called \" << feature->name() << \" at \"\n     << feature->location().latitude()/kCoordFactor_ << \", \"\n     << feature->location().longitude()/kCoordFactor_ << std::endl;\n\n```\n\n##### Streaming RPCs[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#streaming-rpcs)\nNow let’s look at our streaming methods. If you’ve already read [Creating the server](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#server) some of this may look very familiar - streaming RPCs are implemented in a similar way on both sides. Here’s where we call the server-side streaming method `ListFeatures`, which returns a stream of geographical `Feature`s:\n```\nstd::unique_ptr<ClientReader<Feature> > reader(\n  stub_->ListFeatures(&context, rect));\nwhile (reader->Read(&feature)) {\n std::cout << \"Found feature called \"\n      << feature.name() << \" at \"\n      << feature.location().latitude()/kCoordFactor_ << \", \"\n      << feature.location().longitude()/kCoordFactor_ << std::endl;\n}\nStatus status = reader->Finish();\n\n```\n\nInstead of passing the method a context, request, and response, we pass it a context and request and get a `ClientReader` object back. The client can use the `ClientReader` to read the server’s responses. We use the `ClientReader`s `Read()` method to repeatedly read in the server’s responses to a response protocol buffer object (in this case a `Feature`) until there are no more messages: the client needs to check the return value of `Read()` after each call. If `true`, the stream is still good and it can continue reading; if `false` the message stream has ended. Finally, we call `Finish()` on the stream to complete the call and get our RPC status.\nThe client-side streaming method `RecordRoute` is similar, except there we pass the method a context and response object and get back a `ClientWriter`.\n```\nstd::unique_ptr<ClientWriter<Point> > writer(\n  stub_->RecordRoute(&context, &stats));\nfor (int i = 0; i < kPoints; i++) {\n const Feature& f = feature_list_[feature_distribution(generator)];\n std::cout << \"Visiting point \"\n      << f.location().latitude()/kCoordFactor_ << \", \"\n      << f.location().longitude()/kCoordFactor_ << std::endl;\n if (!writer->Write(f.location())) {\n  // Broken stream.\n  break;\n }\n std::this_thread::sleep_for(std::chrono::milliseconds(\n   delay_distribution(generator)));\n}\nwriter->WritesDone();\nStatus status = writer->Finish();\nif (status.IsOk()) {\n std::cout << \"Finished trip with \" << stats.point_count() << \" points\\n\"\n      << \"Passed \" << stats.feature_count() << \" features\\n\"\n      << \"Travelled \" << stats.distance() << \" meters\\n\"\n      << \"It took \" << stats.elapsed_time() << \" seconds\"\n      << std::endl;\n} else {\n std::cout << \"RecordRoute rpc failed.\" << std::endl;\n}\n\n```\n\nOnce we’ve finished writing our client’s requests to the stream using `Write()`, we need to call `WritesDone()` on the stream to let gRPC know that we’ve finished writing, then `Finish()` to complete the call and get our RPC status. If the status is `OK`, our response object that we initially passed to `RecordRoute()` will be populated with the server’s response.\nFinally, let’s look at our bidirectional streaming RPC `RouteChat()`. In this case, we just pass a context to the method and get back a `ClientReaderWriter`, which we can use to both write and read messages.\n```\nstd::shared_ptr<ClientReaderWriter<RouteNote, RouteNote> > stream(\n  stub_->RouteChat(&context));\n\n```\n\nThe syntax for reading and writing here is exactly the same as for our client-streaming and server-streaming methods. Although each side will always get the other’s messages in the order they were written, both the client and server can read and write in any order — the streams operate completely independently.\n### Try it out![ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/#try-it-out)\nBuild the client and server:\n```\nmake\n\n```\n\nRun the server:\n```\n./route_guide_server --db_path=path/to/route_guide_db.json\n\n```\n\nFrom a different terminal, run the client:\n```\n./route_guide_client --db_path=path/to/route_guide_db.json\n\n```\n\nLast modified November 25, 2024: [Authentication](https://grpc.io/docs/guides/auth/)\n      * [Benchmarking](https://grpc.io/docs/guides/benchmarking/)\n      * [Cancellation](https://grpc.io/docs/guides/cancellation/)\n      * [Compression](https://grpc.io/docs/guides/compression/)\n      * [Custom Backend Metrics](https://grpc.io/docs/guides/custom-backend-metrics/)\n      * [Custom Load Balancing page](https://github.com/grpc/grpc.io/edit/main/content/en/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices.md) [ Create child page](https://github.com/grpc/grpc.io/new/main/content/en/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices.md?filename=change-me.md&value=---%0Atitle%3A+%22Long+Page+Title%22%0AlinkTitle%3A+%22Short+Nav+Title%22%0Aweight%3A+100%0Adescription%3A+%3E-%0A+++++Page+description+for+heading+and+indexes.%0A---%0A%0A%23%23+Heading%0A%0AEdit+this+template+to+create+your+new+page.%0A%0A%2A+Give+it+a+good+name%2C+ending+in+%60.md%60+-+e.g.+%60getting-started.md%60%0A%2A+Edit+the+%22front+matter%22+section+at+the+top+of+the+page+%28weight+controls+how+its+ordered+amongst+other+pages+in+the+same+directory%3B+lowest+number+first%29.%0A%2A+Add+a+good+commit+message+at+the+bottom+of+the+page+%28%3C80+characters%3B+use+the+extended+description+field+for+more+detail%29.%0A%2A+Create+a+new+branch+so+you+can+preview+your+new+file+and+request+a+review+via+Pull+Request.%0A) [ Create documentation issue](https://github.com/grpc/grpc.io/issues/new?title=Best%20Practices) [ Create project issue](https://github.com/grpc/grpc.io/issues/new)\n  * [General](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#general)\n  * [Streaming RPCs](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#streaming-rpcs)\n  * [Callback API Specific](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#callback-api-specific)\n    * [Streaming](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#streaming)\n  * [FAQ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#faq)\n    * [General](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#general-1)\n    * [Callback Streaming API](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#callback-streaming-api)\n\n\n  1. [Docs](https://grpc.io/docs/)\n  2. [Languages](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/)\n  3. [C++](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/)\n  4. [Best Practices for gRPC C++ API and FAQ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/)\n\n\n# Best Practices\n[Contents](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#td-content__toc)\n  * [General](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#general)\n  * [Streaming RPCs](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#streaming-rpcs)\n  * [Callback API Specific](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#callback-api-specific)\n    * [Streaming](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#streaming)\n  * [FAQ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#faq)\n    * [General](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#general-1)\n    * [Callback Streaming API](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#callback-streaming-api)\n\n\n# Best Practices\n## General[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#general)\n  * Please **use the callback API**.\n  * **Look for header files with comments** in [third_party/grpc/include/grpcpp](https://github.com/grpc/grpc/tree/master/include/grpcpp).\n  * **Always set a deadline on RPCs.** Here’s a [blog post](https://grpc.io/blog/deadlines/) with some explanation. It’s harder to do so for long-lasting streaming RPCs, but applications can implement custom logic to add deadlines for messages.\n\n\n## Streaming RPCs[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#streaming-rpcs)\n  * **Read all messages until failure** if you need all sent messages. Read until the reaction is called with bool `ok=false` for callback API or the tag has `ok=false` for the async API, or Read fails for the sync API. This is more reliable than counting messages.\n  * **There can only be one read and one write in flight at a time** This is an API requirement rather than a best practice, but worth mentioning again.\n  * **If your application has a two-way stream of data, use bi-directional streaming rather a client-server and server-client model.** This will allow for consistent load balancing and is better supported in gRPC.\n\n\n## Callback API Specific[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#callback-api-specific)\nIn this section, “operations” here are defined as `StartRead`, `StartWrite` (and variants), and `SendInitialMetadata`. `Finish` is also an operation, but often not relevant to the discussion.\n“Reactions” are defined as the overridable callbacks within the reactor, such as `OnReadDone`, `OnWriteDone`, `OnCancel`, and `OnInitialMetadataDone`. `OnDone` is also a reaction, but as the final reaction, the directions here may not be relevant to it.\nBest practices:\n  * **Reactions should be fast.** Do not do blocking or long-running/heavy weight tasks or sleep. It could impact other RPCs within the process.\n\n\n### Streaming[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#streaming)\n  * **Use Holds for starting operations outside reactions.** If you are starting operations on the client from outside reactions, you may need to use [holds](https://github.com/grpc/grpc/blob/24b050b593a435a625e10672fb9b55e8c6501eea/include/grpcpp/support/client_callback.h#L307). This prevents OnDone() from running until holds are removed, preventing races for final cleanup with outstanding operations if the stream has an error. The `bool ok` value in the reactions will reflect if the stream has ended, and operations started after this will all have `ok=false`.\n  * **Synchronize reactions.** Reactions can run in parallel. For example, `OnReadDone` may run at the same time as `OnWriteDone`. Synchronize accordingly.\n  * **Read until false** Rather than counting number of messages, etc. read until `OnReadDone(ok=false)`.\nOn the server side, note that this requires the client to call writes done, which is recommended. The server side does not have to do anything special - `Finish` signals the end of stream.\nA status sent by the application through the `Finish` call may not be visible to the client until all incoming messages have been consumed.\n\n\n## FAQ[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#faq)\n### General[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#general-1)\n  1. How do I debug gRPC issues?\nSee [troubleshooting](https://github.com/grpc/grpc/blob/master/TROUBLESHOOTING.md).\n\n\n### Callback Streaming API[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/best_practices/#callback-streaming-api)\n  1. Is a client half-close required or expected?\nA client half-close is strongly recommended so that the server can continue to read until `OnReadDone(ok=false)`, which is recommended on both the server and client side. However, it is not required – a server could also always choose to Finish() before consuming all client data.\n  2. How do I cancel operations on the client side?\n[`ClientContext::TryCancel()`](https://github.com/grpc/grpc/blob/24b050b593a435a625e10672fb9b55e8c6501eea/include/grpcpp/client_context.h#L392)\n  3. Does the client need to read the data on the wire before `OnDone` is called?\nThe best practice is always to read until `ok=false` on the client side.\nThe client must read all incoming data before it can see an OK status from server `Finish`. However, an error status such as from cancellation or deadline or stream abort may arrive anytime.\nThere are no guarantees about whether an explicit server `Finish` with an error status will be queued behind server writes or delivered immediately. Therefore, the client should always consume all incoming messages by reading until `ok=false` to guarantee delivery of the status.\nSince messages are not guaranteed to be delivered if the server calls `Finish` with an error status, the error status should not be used to communicate success and further directions to a client; trailing metadata should be used for this purpose instead.\n  4. When is `OnDone` called on the client?\nIt is called when there is a status available for the client (all incoming data is read and the server has called `Finish` OR the status is an error that will be delivered immediately), all user reactions have finished running, and all holds are removed.\n  5. When is `OnDone` called on the server?\nAll reactions must finish running (including `OnCancel` when relevant) and server must have called `Finish`.\n  6. What is “within-reactor flow” vs. “outside-reactor flow” and why does it matter?\nWithin-reactor flow is when operations are started from inside reactions (or the reactor constructor) such as `OnWriteDone` starting another write. These make sense since there can only be one read and one write in flight and starting them from the reaction can help maintain that.\nOutside-reactor flow is when operations on the stream are started from outside reactions. This makes sense since reactions aren’t supposed to block and the application may not be ready to perform its next read or write. Note that outside-reactor flows require the use of holds to synchronize on the client side. Server side uses Finish to synchronize outside-reactor calls; the application should not start more operations after calling Finish.\n  7. What are holds and how and when do I use them?\nThey are used to synchronize when OnDone is called and are only needed with outside-reactor flow is used. Note that holds are only on the client side.\n  8. What if the server calls `Finish` but the client keeps starting new operations, such as with `StartWrite`?\n`OnWriteDone(ok=false)` will be called each time the write is started up until `OnDone` is called.\n  9. How do we know when a reactor can be deleted?\nThe reactor can be deleted in `OnDone`. No methods on the reactor base class may be invoked from `OnDone`, and the reactor object will not be accessed by gRPC after `OnDone` is called. It is the application’s responsibility to ensure no operations are started when OnDone is running.\n  10. Can reactions run at the same time, e.g. can `OnReadInitialMetadataDone` run at the same time as `OnReadDone`?\nYes, most reactions may run in parallel. Only `OnDone` runs by itself as the final operation.\n  11. Is `OnReadInitialMetadataDone` called every time, even if the metadata is empty?\nYes, this is used to communicate to the client that the metadata is empty. As with all reactions, the user application need not override this reaction.\n  12. Is `OnSendInitialMetadataDone` called on the server if the initial metadata goes out with the first write rather than because of an explicit `SendInitialMetadata`?\nNo, it has to be explicitly requested. Implicit calls don’t get callbacks.\n  13. If a client calls `WriteLast`, do they get both `OnWriteDone` and `OnWritesDoneDone` callbacks invoked? What happens if they call `Write(options.set_last_message = true)`?\nIf there’s a payload, only `OnWriteDone()` will be called. `OnWritesDoneDone` will be called only in response to `WritesDone()`.\n  14. Can you call `WritesDone()` while you have a write outstanding (i.e. `OnWriteDone()` hasn’t been called yet)?\nYes, the transport orders these.\n  15. When does `OnReadInitialMetadataDone` get called with `ok=false` for the client?\nThis would be called only if there is an error.\n  16. Can a user call `SendInitialMetadata`, `StartWrite` without waiting for `OnSendInitialMetadataDone` in the middle?\nThis is similar to `StartWrite` and `WritesDone`. We do not need to enforce ordering if the transport orders it, but the user may get callbacks invoked in any order.\n  17. When does server `OnCancel` run?\nNote that this is not specific to streaming. It is called for an out-of-band cancellation, i.e. when there is an error on the stream (such as connection abort), a cancellation requested on the client or server side, or a deadline that expires. It can be used as a signal that the client is no longer processing any data.\nIt may run in parallel with other reactions. Operations called after `OnCancel` or started within `OnCancel` will have their reactions called with `ok=false`.\nNote that `OnCancel` no longer runs if the server calls `Finish` specifying an error status because that is not considered an out-of-band cancellation.\nDepending on ordering, `OnCancel` may or may not run if `Finish` is called. However, `OnDone` is always the final callback.\n  18. Does server still need to call `Finish` if `OnCancel` is run?\nYes, although the status passed to `Finish` is ignored.\n  19. Will `OnDone` still be called if `OnCancel` is called?\nYes, `OnDone` is the final callback and will be called once the server has also called `Finish` and all other reactions have completed running.\n  20. Can the user call additional operations (Start*) after `OnCancel`?\nYes, but they will all have reactions run with `ok=false`. It is not valid to call them after calling server `Finish`.\n  21. When does `IsCancelled()` return true on the `context`?\nThis is set when there is an error on the stream, a cancellation requested on the client or server side, or a deadline that expires. Once b/138186533 is resolved, if this kind of error is the reason why reactions are called with `ok=false`, it will be set _before_ these reactions are called.\n  22. Is there a per operation (e.g. `StartRead`) deadline, or only per RPC?\nIt is only per RPC.\n  23. If a user does `stub->MyBidiStreamRPC(); context->TryCancel()`, does the user still need to call `StartCall`?\nYes, it is required once `stub->MyBidiStreamRPC()` is called.\n  24. Is it legal for the server to call `Finish` while there is a Read or Write outstanding?\nThis is fine for reads and `OnReadDone(ok=false)` is called. Calling `Finish` with a write outstanding is not valid API usage, since a Finish can be considered a final write with no data, which would violate the one-write-in-flight rule.\n  25. What happens when the server calls `Finish` with a read outstanding?\n`OnReadDone(ok=false)` is called.\n  26. Can you start another operation (e.g. read or write) in the client reactor’s `OnDone`?\nNo, that is not a legal use of the API.\n  27. Can you start operations on the server after calling `Finish`?\nThis is not a good practice. However, if you start new operations within reactions, their corresponding reactions will be called with `ok=false`. Starting them with outside-reaction flow is illegal and problematic since the operations may race with `OnDone`.\n\n\nLast modified June 25, 2024: [C++ Best Practices (#1309) (4709643)](https://github.com/grpc/grpc.io/commit/47096434163235a9b1814f43652097d71df6831f)\n[ View page tutorial](https://grpc.io/docs/platforms/android/java/basics/)\n          * [API](https://grpc.io/docs/platforms/android/java/api/ \"API reference\")\n        * page](https://github.com/grpc/grpc.io/new/main/content/en/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart.md?filename=change-me.md&value=---%0Atitle%3A+%22Long+Page+Title%22%0AlinkTitle%3A+%22Short+Nav+Title%22%0Aweight%3A+100%0Adescription%3A+%3E-%0A+++++Page+description+for+heading+and+indexes.%0A---%0A%0A%23%23+Heading%0A%0AEdit+this+template+to+create+your+new+page.%0A%0A%2A+Give+it+a+good+name%2C+ending+in+%60.md%60+-+e.g.+%60getting-started.md%60%0A%2A+Edit+the+%22front+matter%22+section+at+the+top+of+the+page+%28weight+controls+how+its+ordered+amongst+other+pages+in+the+same+directory%3B+lowest+number+first%29.%0A%2A+Add+a+good+commit+message+at+the+bottom+of+the+page+%28%3C80+characters%3B+use+the+extended+description+field+for+more+detail%29.%0A%2A+Create+a+new+branch+so+you+can+preview+your+new+file+and+request+a+review+via+Pull+Request.%0A) [Run!](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#run)\n    * [What’s next](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#whats-next)\n\n\n  1. [Docs](https://grpc.io/docs/)\n  2. [Languages](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/)\n  3. [C++](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/)\n  4. [Quick start](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/)\n\n\n# Quick start\nThis guide gets you started with gRPC in C++ with a simple working example.\n[Contents](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#td-content__toc)\n  *     * [Build and locally install gRPC and Protocol Buffers](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#install-grpc)\n      * [Setup](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#setup)\n      * [Install cmake](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#install-cmake)\n      * [Install other required tools](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#install-other-required-tools)\n      * [Clone the `grpc` repo](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#clone-the-grpc-repo)\n      * [Build and install gRPC and Protocol Buffers](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#build-and-install-grpc-and-protocol-buffers)\n    * [Build the example](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#build-the-example)\n    * [Try it!](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#try-it)\n    * [Update the gRPC service](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#update-the-grpc-service)\n    * [Regenerate gRPC code](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#regenerate-grpc-code)\n    * [Update and run the application](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#update-and-run-the-application)\n      * [Update the server](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#update-the-server)\n      * [Update the client](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#update-the-client)\n      * [Run!](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#run)\n    * [What’s next](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#whats-next)\n\n\n# Quick start\nThis guide gets you started with gRPC in C++ with a simple working example.\nIn the C++ world, there’s no universally accepted standard for managing project dependencies. You need to build and install gRPC before building and running this quick start’s Hello World example.\n### Build and locally install gRPC and Protocol Buffers[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#install-grpc)\nThe steps in the section explain how to build and locally install gRPC and Protocol Buffers using `cmake`. If you’d rather use [bazel](https://www.bazel.build), see [Building from source](https://github.com/grpc/grpc/blob/master/BUILDING.md#build-from-source).\n#### Setup[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#setup)\nChoose a directory to hold locally installed packages. This page assumes that the environment variable `MY_INSTALL_DIR` holds this directory path. For example:\n  * Linux / macOS\n\n\n```\nexport MY_INSTALL_DIR=$HOME/.local\n\n```\n\nEnsure that the directory exists:\n```\nmkdir -p $MY_INSTALL_DIR\n\n```\n\nAdd the local `bin` folder to your path variable, for example:\n```\nexport PATH=\"$MY_INSTALL_DIR/bin:$PATH\"\n\n```\n\n  * Windows\n\n\n```\nset MY_INSTALL_DIR=%USERPROFILE%\\cmake\n\n```\n\nEnsure that the directory exists:\n```\nmkdir %MY_INSTALL_DIR%\n\n```\n\nAdd the local `bin` folder to your path variable, for example:\n```\nset PATH=%PATH%;$MY_INSTALL_DIR\\bin\n\n```\n\n#### Install cmake[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#install-cmake)\nYou need version 3.16 or later of `cmake`. Install it by following these instructions if you don’t have it:\n  * Linux\n```\nsudo apt install -y cmake\n\n```\n\n  * macOS:\n```\nbrew install cmake\n\n```\n\n  * Windows:\n```\nchoco install cmake\n\n```\n\n  * For general `cmake` installation instructions, see [Installing CMake](https://cmake.org/install).\n\n\nCheck the version of `cmake`:\n```\ncmake --version\ncmake version 3.30.3\n\n```\n\nUnder Linux, the version of the system-wide `cmake` can often be too old. You can install a more recent version into your local installation directory as follows:\n```\nwget -q -O cmake-linux.sh https://github.com/Kitware/CMake/releases/download/v3.30.3/cmake-3.30.3-linux-x86_64.sh\nsh cmake-linux.sh -- --skip-license --prefix=$MY_INSTALL_DIR\nrm cmake-linux.sh\n\n```\n\n#### Install other required tools[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#install-other-required-tools)\nInstall the basic tools required to build gRPC:\n  * Linux\n```\nsudo apt install -y build-essential autoconf libtool pkg-config\n\n```\n\n  * macOS:\n```\nbrew install autoconf automake libtool pkg-config\n\n```\n\n\n\n#### Clone the `grpc` repo[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#clone-the-grpc-repo)\nClone the `grpc` repo and its submodules:\n```\ngit clone --recurse-submodules -b v1.73.0 --depth 1 --shallow-submodules https://github.com/grpc/grpc\n\n```\n\n#### Build and install gRPC and Protocol Buffers[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#build-and-install-grpc-and-protocol-buffers)\nWhile not mandatory, gRPC applications usually leverage [Protocol Buffers](https://developers.google.com/protocol-buffers) for service definitions and data serialization, and the example code uses [proto3](https://protobuf.dev/programming-guides/proto3).\nThe following commands build and locally install gRPC and Protocol Buffers:\n  * Linux & macOS\n```\ncd grpc\nmkdir -p cmake/build\npushd cmake/build\ncmake -DgRPC_INSTALL=ON \\\n   -DgRPC_BUILD_TESTS=OFF \\\n   -DCMAKE_CXX_STANDARD=17 \\\n   -DCMAKE_INSTALL_PREFIX=$MY_INSTALL_DIR \\\n   ../..\nmake -j 4\nmake install\npopd\n\n```\n\n  * Windows\n```\nmkdir \"cmake\\build\"\npushd \"cmake\\build\"\ncmake -DgRPC_INSTALL=ON -DgRPC_BUILD_TESTS=OFF -DCMAKE_CXX_STANDARD=17 -DCMAKE_INSTALL_PREFIX=%MY_INSTALL_DIR% ..\\..\ncmake --build . --config Release --target install -j 4\npopd\n\n```\n\n\n\n#### Important\nWe **strongly** encourage you to install gRPC _locally_ — using an appropriately set `CMAKE_INSTALL_PREFIX` — because there is no easy way to uninstall gRPC after you’ve installed it globally.\nMore information:\n  * You can find a complete set of instructions for building gRPC C++ in [Building from source](https://github.com/grpc/grpc/blob/master/BUILDING.md#build-from-source).\n  * For general instructions on how to add gRPC as a dependency to your C++ project, see [Start using gRPC C++](https://github.com/grpc/grpc/tree/master/src/cpp#to-start-using-grpc-c).\n\n\n### Build the example[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#build-the-example)\nThe example code is part of the `grpc` repo source, which you cloned as part of the steps of the previous section.\n  1. Change to the example’s directory:\n```\ncd examples/cpp/helloworld\n\n```\n\n  2. Build the example using `cmake`:\n     * Linux & macOS\n```\nmkdir -p cmake/build\npushd cmake/build\ncmake -DCMAKE_PREFIX_PATH=$MY_INSTALL_DIR ../..\nmake -j 4\n\n```\n\n     * Windows\n```\nmkdir \"cmake\\build\"\npushd \"cmake\\build\"\ncmake -DCMAKE_INSTALL_PREFIX=%MY_INSTALL_DIR% ..\\..\ncmake --build . --config Release -j 4\npopd\n\n```\n\n#### Note\n**Getting build failures?** Most issues, at this point, are the result of a faulty installation. Make sure you have the right version of `cmake`, and carefully recheck your installation.\n\n\n### Try it![ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#try-it)\nRun the example from the example **build** directory `examples/cpp/helloworld/cmake/build`:\n  1. Run the server:\n```\n./greeter_server\n\n```\n\n  2. From a different terminal, run the client and see the client output:\n```\n./greeter_client\nGreeter received: Hello world\n\n```\n\n\n\nCongratulations! You’ve just run a client-server application with gRPC.\n### Update the gRPC service[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#update-the-grpc-service)\nNow let’s look at how to update the application with an extra method on the server for the client to call. Our gRPC service is defined using protocol buffers; you can find out lots more about how to define a service in a `.proto` file in [Introduction to gRPC](https://grpc.io/docs/what-is-grpc/introduction/) and [Basics tutorial](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/). For now all you need to know is that both the server and the client stub have a `SayHello()` RPC method that takes a `HelloRequest` parameter from the client and returns a `HelloReply` from the server, and that this method is defined like this:\n```\n// The greeting service definition.\nservice Greeter { // Sends a greeting\n rpc SayHello (HelloRequest) returns (HelloReply) {}}// The request message containing the user's name.\nmessage HelloRequest { string name = 1;}// The response message containing the greetings\nmessage HelloReply { string message = 1;}\n```\n\nOpen [examples/protos/helloworld.proto](https://github.com/grpc/grpc/blob/v1.73.0/examples/protos/helloworld.proto) and add a new `SayHelloAgain()` method, with the same request and response types:\n```\n// The greeting service definition.\nservice Greeter { // Sends a greeting\n rpc SayHello (HelloRequest) returns (HelloReply) {} // Sends another greeting\n rpc SayHelloAgain (HelloRequest) returns (HelloReply) {}}// The request message containing the user's name.\nmessage HelloRequest { string name = 1;}// The response message containing the greetings\nmessage HelloReply { string message = 1;}\n```\n\nRemember to save the file!\n### Regenerate gRPC code[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#regenerate-grpc-code)\nBefore you can use the new service method, you need to recompile the updated proto file.\nFrom the example **build** directory `examples/cpp/helloworld/cmake/build`, run:\n  * Linux & macOS\n\n\n```\nmake -j 4\n\n```\n\n  * Windows\n\n\n```\ncmake --build . --config Release -j 4\n\n```\n\nThis regenerates `helloworld.pb.{h,cc}` and `helloworld.grpc.pb.{h,cc}`, which contains the generated client and server classes, as well as classes for populating, serializing, and retrieving our request and response types.\n### Update and run the application[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#update-and-run-the-application)\nYou have new generated server and client code, but you still need to implement and call the new method in the human-written parts of our example application.\n#### Update the server[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#update-the-server)\nOpen `greeter_server.cc` from the example’s root directory. Implement the new method like this:\n```\nclass GreeterServiceImpl final : public Greeter::Service {\n Status SayHello(ServerContext* context, const HelloRequest* request,\n         HelloReply* reply) override {\n   // ...\n }\n Status SayHelloAgain(ServerContext* context, const HelloRequest* request,\n            HelloReply* reply) override {\n  std::string prefix(\"Hello again \");\n  reply->set_message(prefix + request->name());\n  return Status::OK;\n }\n};\n\n```\n\n#### Update the client[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#update-the-client)\nA new `SayHelloAgain()` method is now available in the stub. We’ll follow the same pattern as for the already present `SayHello()` and add a new `SayHelloAgain()` method to `GreeterClient`:\n```\nclass GreeterClient {\n public:\n // ...\n std::string SayHello(const std::string& user) {\n   // ...\n }\n std::string SayHelloAgain(const std::string& user) {\n  // Follows the same pattern as SayHello.\n  HelloRequest request;\n  request.set_name(user);\n  HelloReply reply;\n  ClientContext context;\n  // Here we can use the stub's newly available method we just added.\n  Status status = stub_->SayHelloAgain(&context, request, &reply);\n  if (status.ok()) {\n   return reply.message();\n  } else {\n   std::cout << status.error_code() << \": \" << status.error_message()\n        << std::endl;\n   return \"RPC failed\";\n  }\n }\n\n```\n\nFinally, invoke this new method in `main()`:\n```\nint main(int argc, char** argv) {\n // ...\n std::string reply = greeter.SayHello(user);\n std::cout << \"Greeter received: \" << reply << std::endl;\n reply = greeter.SayHelloAgain(user);\n std::cout << \"Greeter received: \" << reply << std::endl;\n return 0;\n}\n\n```\n\n#### Run![ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#run)\nRun the client and server like you did before. Execute the following commands from the example **build** directory `examples/cpp/helloworld/cmake/build`:\n  1. Build the client and server after having made changes:\n     * Linux & macOS\n```\nmake -j 4\n\n```\n\n     * Windows\n```\ncmake --build . --config Release -j 4\n\n```\n\n  2. Run the server:\n```\n./greeter_server\n\n```\n\n  3. On a different terminal, run the client:\n```\n./greeter_client\n\n```\n\nYou’ll see the following output:\n```\nGreeter received: Hello world\nGreeter received: Hello again world\n\n```\n\n\n\n#### Note\nInterested in an **asynchronous** version of the client and server? You’ll find the `greeter_async_{client,server}.cc` files in the [example’s source directory](https://github.com/grpc/grpc/tree/master/examples/cpp/helloworld).\n### What’s next[ ](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart/#whats-next)\n  * Learn how gRPC works in [Introduction to gRPC](https://grpc.io/docs/what-is-grpc/introduction/) and [Core concepts](https://grpc.io/docs/what-is-grpc/core-concepts/).\n  * Work through the [Basics tutorial](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/basics/).\n  * Explore the [API reference](https://grpc.io/docs/languages/cpp/api).\n\n\nLast modified April 19, 2025: [Update quickstart.md (#1420) (fe6a359)](https://github.com/grpc/grpc.io/commit/fe6a3593cb07e0c35d3833e4e4824ec1c670fec3)\n[ View page source](https://github.com/grpc/grpc.io/tree/main/content/en/docs/languages/cpp/quickstart.md)
